CHAP.  XXVII.

OF  IDENTITY  ANTD  DIVERSITY".

§  I.    Wherein  Identity  corjifts.

ANOTHER  occafion  the  mind  often  takes  of  com

iKtering  any  tiling  as  exiiting  at  any  determined  time
and  place,  we  compare  it  with  its  felf-exiiling  at  another
time,  and  thereon  form  the  ideas  of  identity  and  diverfity.
When  we  fee  any  thing  to  be  in  any  place  in  any  in-
ftant  of  time,  we  are  fure  (be  it  what  it  will)  that  it  is

confifts  identity^  when  the  ideas  it  is  attributed  to  vary

we  confider  -their  former  exiftence,  and  to  which  we

exifted  fuch  a  time  in  fuch  a  place,  which  it  was  certain
at  that  inftant  was  the  fame  with  itfelf,  and  no  other.
From  whence  it  follows,  that  one  thing  cannot  have  two
beginnings  of  exiitence,  nor  two  things  one  beginning  ;
it  being  impoffible  for  two  things  of  the  fame  kind  to
he  or  exift  in  the  fame  inftant,  in  the  very  fame  place,
or  one  and  the  fame  thing  in  different  places.  That
therefore  that  had  one  beginning,  is  the  fame  thing  ;
and  that  which  had  a  different  beginning  in  time  and
place  from  that,  is  not  the  fame,  but  diverfe.  That
which^has^  made  die  difficulty  about  this  relation,  has
been  the  little  care  and  attention  ufed  in  having  preclfe
notions  of  the  things  to  which  it  is  attributed.

§  2.     Identity  of  Sub/lancet.

$  3.    Liftituted.

THIRDLY,  Sometimes  the  foundation  of  confidering

§  4.      Moral.

FOURTHLY,  There  is  another  fort  of  relation,  which  is

have  to  a  rule  to  which  they  are  referred,  and  by  which

§  5 .      Moral  Good  and  Evil.

GOOD  and  evil,  as  hath  been  mown,  B.  II.  Ch.  20.  §  2.
and  Ch  21.  §  42.  are  nothing  but  pleafure  or  pain,  or
that  which  occafions,  or  procures  pleafure  or  pain  to  us.
Moral  good  and  evil  then  is  only  the  conformity  or  dif-
agreement  of  our  voluntary  actions  tofome  law,  where
by  good  or  evil  is  drawn  on  us  by  the  will  and  power
of  the  law-  maker  ;  which  good  and  evil,  pleafure  or
pain,  attending  our  obfervance,  or  breach  of  the  law,  by
the  decree  of  the  law-maker,  is  that  we  call  reward  and
puniihment.

THE  laws  that  men  generally  refer  their  actions  to,  to
